improve the links between Prague and Berlin in the framework of IV. the trans-European transport corridor Berlin - Istanbul - an international objective.
According to the programme, construction of the section running through the Czech Central Mountains was to be completed in June 2008 and construction of the border section No. 0807 in June 2007.
It must be added here that the completion date is not identical to the commissioning date.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains is now at the stage of an official territorial decision.
Environmental activists have challenged this decision with lawsuits.
There is no final decision on the actions.
Therefore, it cannot be excluded that the section running through the Czech Central Mountains will be completed several years later than mentioned in the programme documentation.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed to postpone the completion of section 0805 for 2010.
As a result of these facts, the construction of the border section did not achieve the national objective - to improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The MSD made an estimate of capital expenditure on the border section D 8 No 0807 and section No 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains in 1999 - 2001.
The estimate in this capital expenditure of the included investment costs is about €639 million combined for both sections 0805 and 0807.
Table 2: Economic assessment results (EIRR = internal rate of return)
All considered motorways and speed roads opened, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 18 000 vehicles per day in 2010
All considered motorways and speed roads opened, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 22 000 vehicles per day in 2010
All considered motorways and speed roads opened, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 25 000 vehicles per day in 2010
The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and section through the Czech Central Mountains would be completed in time.
At the time of inspection, however, it is clear that the section through the Czech Central Mountains will not be open at the original date.
This will reduce the above mentioned EIRR values.
The cost of both sections, excluding VAT, as at 31 December 2005, was €897 million, i.e. higher by 27.4% than the level of costs with which the 2001 economic analysis was carried out.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will be run on a different, more costly route, which would affect the aforementioned economic viability threshold.
The economic analysis showed that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable with a traffic intensity of at least 18 000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but a study addressing the problem of roadside routes for the missing section across the Czech Central Mountains foresees a traffic intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, pushing the investment below the economic viability threshold.
In the case of higher loads of roadways for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains, there is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which were not foreseen in the original economic analysis.
The above-mentioned decrease in revenues and cost increases means that the EIRR that the construction of the border section D 8 was to have had will not be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
Also during the construction of the A 17 motorway there was a similar excess of costs as in the case of these two sections on D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the federal road development plan for long-distance transport for 1992, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany envisaged a cost of about €320 million.
The resulting cost-benefit ratio was 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have soared to about €670 million and the cost-benefit ratio has fallen by more than half.
In addition to the parallel checks carried out jointly with the SAO, the BRH audited the preparation and construction of motorway A 17 in 2003 and 2005.
In doing so, the BRH found that the competent approval authority had requested an off-road tunnel for Section A 17, which was not needed.
During the control operation, BRH managed to convince the Federal Ministry and the German Administration for Road Construction that the tunnel was not necessary.
This resulted in savings of around €10 million and €0.15 million in annual operating costs alone.
When assessing the first section A 17, BRH found that the construction project was not properly prepared.
This is illustrated by the following example:
At the preparatory stage, the Road Construction Administration did not survey the rock floor conditions in the Dölzschen rocks because the site was difficult to access.
This was done only after the tunnel strike machine hit the rock.
Tests showed that the rocks inside were more stale than anticipated during the selection process.
Therefore, costly measures such as anchoring, injection and stone protection nets had to be taken, requiring around €3.3 million.
The Directorate of Roads and Motorways had to bear the costs not only of other extraordinary operations, such as the safety measures in the Dölzschen rocks, but also of measures that would not have been needed if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), additional training of drilling staff (€0.5 million) and rising wages and expenditure on goods due to exceeding the duration of the project (€7 million).
The cost overruns thus amounted to around €10.7 million, which was not matched by any added value.
On the basis of lessons learned from these and other controlled projects, BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter guidance in its supervision of road construction work.
The Federal Ministry should give the Directorate of Roads and Motorways the necessary incentive to implement quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and SAO Cooperation fulfilled the intention of both audit institutions.
BRH gained control knowledge about the preparation, commissioning and progress of the border bridge construction.
The two Supreme Audit Institutions have built a database of bridges and tunnels prices on which to rely on future controls.
The SAO found that the main building-technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the route of the D 8 motorway are not different from the parameters of A 17.
The auditors of the two institutions involved in the cooperation have gained new experience which can be used for further inspections.

Coordinated control of funding for projects implemented under the Operational Programme of Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013
INTRODUCTION
The emerging community of the people of Europe is driving cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
Let this Community give an opening statement on this joint report of the two supreme audit institutions of the neighbouring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral, often a remnant of the darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help mitigate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers and to further help develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: some cover coastal areas, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most programmes are bilateral, but some include more than two neighbouring countries. "
The programmes last seven years and are allocated a total of EUR 5.7 billion from the EU budget.
"The programmes include the following activities: repair and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycling paths and bridges, investment in cross-border waste management systems, healthcare facilities, research centres, flood prevention measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of common services for the local population, employment advice, creation of thematic networks and cluster
Support from the programme is granted on condition that organisations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to implement projects based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself is over.
The funding shall be provided on condition that project partners from both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four features: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing. "
Within the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of the project activities are clearly defined and distributed to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership include: joint selection of the lead partner, joint preparation of the project and application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, assumption of overall responsibility for the implementation of the project by the lead partner, total payments of support are received by the lead partner and their transfer to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects carried out on the territory of regions and regions adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Polish Republic 2007-2013 (OPPS CR-POL).
€ 258 187 467 has been allocated for the implementation of the operational programme.
The total amount included a contribution from the European Funds (ERDF) totalling € 219 459 344 (85%), which must be exhausted by the end of 2015.
The financial data on the use of EU funds from the Operational Programme of Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Polish Republic 2007-2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the following table:
The Agreement on Cooperation between the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the Presidents of both offices on 27 February 2013 following previous mutual meetings at the Heads of the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia held in Lovasbereny, Hungary in September ary 2012.
The aim of the cooperation was to check whether the implementation of the operational programme is in accordance with the legislation and binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system is reliable, whether the objectives of the operational programme are achieved and whether the selected projects are implemented in accordance with the specified conditions of the support provided.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel check whereby, on the basis of their statutory mandate, the audit authorities independently examined topics the content of which had been discussed together in advance.
IRING segmentISintent  EU blank
The audit carried out also included elements of the performance audit.
Selected topics were examined on a sample of projects by project partners on both sides of the border.
The checks carried out resulted in audit findings published in the usual manner by both offices.
On the basis of the two audit conclusions set out in the Annex, this joint report was established.
The selected joint control sample included six projects (Annex 1) falling within aid area 1.1 - Enhancing transport accessibility and aid area 2.2 - Support for tourism development.
Prior to the start of the audit, the use of funds from these two support areas represented more than 60% of the funds reimbursed to the beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects within the whole OPPS CR-POL.
IRING segmentISintent  EU blank
In addition to checking the joint sample of six projects, the SAO inspected another 13 projects and NIK checked two more (all in the area of aid 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to the beneficiaries of the ERDF aid, which were controlled by the SAO and NIK, totalled €25,362,174 (SAO) and €8,450,784 (NIK).
The audited amount represented 41% of all funds paid to aid beneficiaries in aid areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONTROL CONCLUSIONS
Both checks independently carried out by the SAO on the Czech side and NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of irregularities identified did not exceed the level of relevance.
The implementation of OPPS CR-POL, which includes the operation of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The audited persons who were part of the management and control system have taken steps to reduce the risk of misuse of funds and the non-use of ERDF funds.
Evidence of the effectiveness of the implemented projects under the operational programme is the high level of achievement of the set objectives, which was also supported by the results of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of the projects.
SUMMARY OF THE RESULTS OF THE INSPECTION
AND. Evaluation of the management and control system
The coordinated approach enabled the control of the management and control system of OPPS CR-POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry of Local Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry of Regional Development POL) and the controllers (Centre for Regional Development and Province).
At the same time, the coordinated audit enabled the evaluation of the tasks and functions carried out by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the control focused on the activities carried out in connection with the setting up and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in relation to setting the objectives of the programme and monitoring progress made, evaluation activities and annual reports, assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, by drawing on the Programme's resources and fulfilling the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on subsidies; in addition to the control activities of the managing authority, the setting of the performance of the activities and the output from the audits of the delegated audit body and the reporting and monitoring system of irregularities and their compliance, coordination of tasks and cooperation between the institutions involved in OPPS CR-POL has also been examined.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and the drawing up of control plans at the site of project implementation have been examined and the evaluation of project applications carried out by independent evaluators has been assessed.
The verification of the control system included a review of administrative and on-the-spot checks, which allowed the authorisation of, for example, the supply of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, and the consistency of the disbursement of such funds and associated activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant shortcomings were identified by the SAO's audit of the publication of correct, complete and up-to-date information on the subsidies provided.
The evaluation and approval of projects identified a risk of lack of transparency in this process.
Other deficiencies were identified in the monitoring indicator settings and in the irregularity reporting system settings.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
The irregularities identified by NIK concerning the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to infringements of applicable laws and rules were not assessed as significant.
These irregularities only affected individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the entire OPPS CR-POL.
Despite the irregularities identified, NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The check did not show a contradiction between the indicators and objectives set for each project and those defined by the operational programme.
All audited projects achieved expected results, financed activities were implemented and indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the project applications of the operational programme were met.
The audit conclusions are consistent with the outcome of the survey of social evaluation of the implementation of the projects described in the text below.
An important aspect to which the SAO and NIK have paid attention in their conclusions is the limited possibility of an objective evaluation of the achievement of the projected results of each project, taking into account the objectives of each project and the objectives of the entire operational programme.
On the initiative of the Supreme Chamber of Auditors, an opinion poll was conducted in summer 2013 at the place of implementation of projects selected for the joint control sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nyse (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the investigation was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both with the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project implemented by Polish project partners and with the Czech public in relation to the part of the project implemented in the Czech Republic.
The survey concerned in particular the suitability, quality, exchange of information, use of funds and social function of the projects, including investments made.
The number of respondents per project is set out in Annex 2.
The assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall societal evaluation of the projects was good and amounted to 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), which means that the results of the projects were positively perceived by the majority of respondents both with respect to the objectives and with respect to the other parameters set out in the project applications.
According to the respondents, the projects examined fulfilled the criteria expressed in the operational programme.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourist infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and renewed contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of responses indicated a distinction between the actual level of achievement of the objectives/parameters (actual impact rate) and the level expected by respondents.
This difference applies to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the views of the participants of the panel discussions (target groups) carried out in the context of the NIK check on the Polish side, who pointed out the need to implement the projects and confirmed their positive public acceptance and fulfilment of the expected results.
C. Evaluation of the legality of audited projects
The compatibility of the project's substantive and timely implementation with the timetable and content of the project application has been examined for the beneficiaries, further verify the achievement of the stated objectives and purpose of the project, the fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, management of changes in projects, handling of possible project revenues, fulfilment of the obligation to separate accounting records of project transactions, compliance with the ban on funding for projects from other EU sources, implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions; the implementation of mandatory publicity on, in particular, the dissemination of awareness of sources of funding for EU projects; compliance with the eligibility conditions for project expenditure, selection of suppliers and verification of the performance delivered on the basis of the procurement awarded.
The total financial value of the shortcomings of the qualifying SAO and NIK as expenditure ineligible for EU funding was found at €85,094.
However, this amount did not exceed the determined level of materiality related to the total audited amount for the sample of projects.
Despite the observed irregularities, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of selected projects positively by the beneficiaries.
In most cases, the obligations arising from the conditions of the aid to the beneficiary were properly fulfilled.
Funds for the implementation of controlled projects have been spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
Nevertheless, seven of the eight aid beneficiaries audited by NIK were found to have irregularities in the fulfilment of material and financial obligations.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and concerned in particular inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, which did not have an impact on the choice of contractor or the supply of contractually agreed works.
In only one of the NIK-controlled projects, the identified irregularity related to non-compliance with reporting obligations can be assessed as an ineligible expenditure of PLN 43,003 (of which EUR 8,231 financed by the ERDF).
On the other hand, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure of CZK 2,098 053 in three of the 19 projects audited (of which €76,863 was financed by the ERDF).
The irregularities included the failure to deliver the material and work paid for or the expense of modernising the property owned by a person who was not linked to the project either as a beneficiary of the aid or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATIONS
The joint recommendation of the SAO and the NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system in particular with a view to the consistent establishment of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational programme.
In general, it is also necessary to endeavour to identify indicators at programme level as well as projects that allow more reliable verification of the implementation of the impacts identified.
The notes and recommendations resulting from the checks carried out on both sides of the border are included in the SAO and NIK separate audit conclusions on CDs.
COMMUNIQUÉ
The report was prepared in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Polish Republic.
Public works procurement and prevention of corruption
Joint report on parallel control carried out by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
Public procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and the private sphere.
The role of the State is to use public resources as efficiently and economically as possible and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor in the success of the single economic area.
The EU has set limits in its rules, beyond which the contracting authority must meet certain requirements to guarantee a wide and unrestricted competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts that are below EU value limits, regulation of their procurement remains the responsibility of individual countries.
Moreover, public procurement is prone to corruption with a consequent high potential for harm.
Therefore, contracting entities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair favouritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI - SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI - BRH) agreed to carry out parallel checks on procurement procedures and the prevention of corruption in land and transport structures.
Cooperation was also focused on European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
The controls included the award of contracts below European thresholds in order to affect the area of corruption prevention.
The audit findings of the two supreme audit institutions are summarised in a joint report, inter alia addressed to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Control areas and controlled institutions
The evaluation of BRH is based on the blanket checks of the above mentioned areas carried out by the German construction administrations responsible for federal transport and land construction between 2011 and 2012.
For example, highway construction and large-scale building projects have been evaluated.
The BRH also included findings from past inspections in the evaluation.
The SAO's evaluation is based on inspections carried out between 2008 and 2012, which focused on the construction of roads, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other buildings in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, the award of federal public contracts is mostly carried out by regional construction administrations.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement in the field of transport infrastructure is the responsibility of the state institution for road and railway construction.
In the field of civil engineering, public procurement is carried out by the beneficiaries of the budget.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used in the various stages of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation should take into account differences in public procurement legislation and the specificity of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and sub-legal standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany shall be governed by budgetary law and by the relevant administrative acts.
The internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details concerning the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called procurement manuals of the federal government, in which the requirements are developed or formulated in greater detail with regard to legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation concerning contracts with a value above and below European limits.
The procurement law has undergone substantial changes in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened transparency in procurement procedures, opened up room for greater competition and reduced opportunities for corruption in the procurement process.
Fundamental aspects of procurement law in both countries
As regards European procurement procedures, open and restricted procedures must be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts under the European limit are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, limited call for participation, negotiated procedure), but these are practically identical to the above mentioned conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and in a call for competition, any candidate may submit a tender.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized firms.
This is mainly achieved by dividing the total public contract into commercial specific parts and dividing it into sub-contracts.
The award of contracts divided into these parts gives the opportunity to participate in the procedure as many candidates as possible.
The two characteristics of German procurement law represent over time tested instruments for obtaining good value for money.
More than 330 000 construction companies with 1.9 million employees are registered in Germany.
In the Czech Republic it is necessary to apply the conditions of European procedures even in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
The exception is a simplified sub-standard procedure where the contracting authority may use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which a contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
The current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between open procedures and restricted procedures.
85 000 construction companies are registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1 900 have 20 or more employees.
Total volume of contracting authorities' public works contracts and share of each type of procedure in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction work in Germany amounted to 7,675 billion.
CZK, of which CZK 1,138 billion (15%) was the share of public contracts, which included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities in addition to those awarded by the Federal Government.
The total volume of projects of federal road and land structures (without construction of railways and water structures) reached CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of federal land works and transport construction contracts was as follows: 80.7% open procedures and public calls; 12.7% narrower management and closer challenges;
6,6% negotiated procedures and direct procurement; 33.2% is the share of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic reached CZK 118.6 billion in 2011, which was about 25% of the total volume of construction work.
In 2009 it amounted to CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total volume.
Structure of procurement types in 2011: 65.1% open procedures; 14.3% restricted driving; 9,5% of the negotiated procedure; 8,4% simplified procedures for sub-prime contracts; 2,7% other proceedings.
These data include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
These figures show that: parallel checks in both countries focused on a substantial part of the public procurement procedures; the prevention of corruption, which must still be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open meaning. procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of the main results
The SAO and the BRH carried out parallel checks on the award of European works contracts with an emphasis on preventing corruption in their countries.
The checks focused on the comparison of legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public procurement in the field of land and transport construction.
Key findings are also presented:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national rights of both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
The prevention of corruption is not only the aim of the EU Directive, but the national procurement rules of both countries, which differ in part in terms of focus and detail.
In both countries, the essential requirement to be met by the procurement documents is to include all the information necessary for the preparation of tenders.
The specifications shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements limiting competition.
The procurement documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic the problem is the insufficient definition of the subject of the contract.
In Germany, dividing contracts into parts where possible is required in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to the findings of BRH, this requirement is respected in Germany.
Audits of the SAO have shown that in the Czech Republic very large public works in the field of road construction are awarded without being divided into parts, or divided into parts, but only on very significant volumes. This can distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
These market potential constraints may result in significant cost increases and risk unserious price behaviour and inadmissible conventions.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting entities, under certain conditions, to award additional works if the need arises only after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
The requirements for the correct identification of the type of procurement procedure are met in both countries.
In the Czech Republic, according to the applicable regulations, contracting entities may arbitrarily designate one of the open procurement procedures, namely an open procedure or a restricted procedure.
The German rules on procurement and budgetary law give priority to open procedures for both European tenders and national tenders, known as public calls for competition.
Open procurement procedures in both countries allow a larger range of candidates to submit a tender and also reduce the risk of corruption by making it difficult to negotiate prices illegally between candidates.
At the same time, the best results are achieved in terms of value for money.
In the case of large contracts whose estimated total price is above or above the EU thresholds, the audited contracting entities have always issued a European procurement procedure.
Proper determination of the estimated value of the contract plays a very important role in determining the type of procurement procedure.
In Germany, databases are used for objective pricing of public works in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective tool.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the field of road construction, but they are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a works contract under European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish the preliminary information and the notice of initiation in order to strengthen competition by allowing candidates to prepare in time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
When selecting candidates to participate in a restricted procurement procedure, there is a greater risk of manipulation.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be sufficiently high to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective, non-discriminatory and shall be published sufficiently in advance.
In addition to EU requirements, contracting authorities in both countries should alternate as frequently as possible the candidates who are invited to participate in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany there are some very detailed requirements for preventing the risks of manipulation.
In some cases, the audited contracting entities did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (section 4.4).
In both countries, contracting authorities must ensure during the procurement procedure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between those interested parties and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must be prevented to the extent possible from limiting competition in the form of illegal agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the audited German contracting authorities did not meet the above requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
Labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality requirements are designed to limit the scope for handling and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Inspections in both countries identified only a few cases of irregularities (section 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of tenders is procedurally regulated in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive but have not always been respected by the audited contracting entities.
For example, contracting entities transmitted their tenders to third parties for consultation without prior assessment, without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
The tenders were thus insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulations (point 4.7).
The intended award of the contract must be subject to legal protection of enterprises are to be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule is also designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The tender documentation for the audited contracting entities in both countries was not always complete or fully credible.
In the case of several procurement procedures, transparency requirements applied at European level have not always been adequately complied with in the minutes of the procedure.
In addition, some of the deficiencies identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the dossier.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly fulfilled their reporting obligations (point 4.9).
Summary of the guiding principles of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption
European procurement law
The European Parliament and the Council of the European Union have adopted, inter alia, a directive on the coordination of public works procurement procedures to ensure free competition in the EU, supplies and services to ensure the free movement of goods and services and the Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to review procedures for the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (Review Procedures Directive), the two Directives being transposed into national law.
The general procurement principles of the EU include in particular: the requirement for a competitive environment; a requirement for equal treatment or non-discrimination; a requirement for transparency.
in addition, the EU procurement principles include the Directive on the coordination of procurement procedures includes provisions relating to construction projects whose total expected value is or exceeds €5 million.
These provisions affect: the contents of the procurement documents; the type of procurement procedure used; the publication of certain information before, during and after the completion of the procedure; compliance with the deadline for receipt of tenders; qualification requirements which may be required of candidates; criteria for selecting the most appropriate tender.
Legal protection of tenderers is governed by the Review Procedures Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of the procurement law, tenderers may have the decision of the contracting authority reviewed in accordance with the examination procedures.
Both countries have transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Prevention of corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
In either country, there is no legal definition of what corruption actually means.
Corruption is generally seen as an abuse of a personal (confidential) position for its own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is prompted by the beneficiaries.
Corruption can hinder competition and cause serious economic losses.
Overall corruption-related damage is estimated to account for 5% of world economic output.
For the EU, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
Where a contracting authority is involved in the contract, conventions limiting competition or allowing dishonest conduct may undermine confidence in the integrity, capacity and overall work of the public administration.
Therefore, prevention of corruption is an essential task for contracting authorities.
Practice and practices must be monitored in particular in areas particularly vulnerable to corruption in order to take adequate measures to prevent it.
The adoption of preventive measures and the implementation of effective controls can significantly reduce the susceptibility of the decision-making process to corruption.
The award of works contracts is generally regarded as particularly vulnerable to corruption.
Measures to prevent corruption
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, which is particularly relevant to active and passive bribery, the reception and offering of significant benefits.
The German Federal Government issued a manual with recommendations on the prevention of corruption in the Federal Administration and according to which the following principles are applied throughout the entire procurement process beyond risk analysis: the preference for open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; separation of planning, procurement and accounting phases; accurate and complete documentation of the procurement procedure.
In addition, other measures preventing corruption have become part of the procurement law.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry of Information Technology introduced a system for monitoring procurement for the construction of federal roads.
With this system, data such as estimated costs, the total value of the contract and the involvement of private projection offices can be fully recorded and analysed in order to identify any irregularities.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, and a level of labour or civil law is also considered.
The legal requirement for transparency, equal treatment and prohibition of any discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were set out by the Government in the "Government Strategy against Corruption 2011 to 2012," updated for 2013 and 2014.
Part of these measures has been incorporated into the Act on Public Procurement and Successive Implementing Regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: lowering the thresholds for small-scale contracts; the obligation to notify the contracting authority ex ante, including the reasons for the contract; a prohibition on limiting the number of shortlisted tenderers and abolishing the general possibility of limiting the number of applicants by drawing lots; the obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; the obligation to publish actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of the checks relating to the subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Procurement documents
The tender documents shall contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate form.
The documentation shall in particular enable the tender price to be processed.
In order to do so, candidates must not be exposed to risks which they could not reckon with.
The tender documents contain specifications in particular, as well as requirements for proving the qualifications of tenderers and criteria for the selection of the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
In particular, European law contains requirements relating to technical specifications.
The specifications must minimise competition and all interested parties must have equal access to them.
The specification must be neutral.
Requirements for specific products are permitted on a very limited scale in the procurement documents.
The basic principle of corruption prevention is that the procurement documents do not contain requirements that might restrict competition.
Furthermore, the documentation should avoid confusion or inaccurate data that create room for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications are generally given without reference to a particular product or brand.
Specifications shall specify the required supplies and services and specify in detail the number of units of work and supply.
In normal cases, prices should be indicated by units of works or supplies (e.g. in CZK/m or in CZK/item).
Defining the work required in terms of its functionality is only permissible in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that specifications need to be drawn up completely and unambiguously.
They shall contain precise quantities and shall not include works and supplies the necessity of which is not entirely obvious.
Payment in the form of lump sums or according to the time worked should be avoided wherever possible.
These parts of the procurement documents shall exclude speculation and manipulation based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must allow each tenderer to prepare his offer with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender shall not be published until the procurement documents are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic there is no obligation for contracting entities to award construction contracts in parts.
The contracting authority may divide the contract into several separate parts, provided that the nature of the subject-matter of such a contract so permits.
Germany requires the application of the rule to divide works into parts, either quantitatively (partial supplies) or according to specific aspects (specialised parts of the contract).
This enables small and medium-sized entities to participate in the competition, increases the number of potential bidders and makes price fixation difficult.
This type of award is usually considered most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, procurement documents must contain evaluation criteria for national and European procurement procedures.
This should inform the tenderer in a comprehensive and exhaustive manner of the criteria on which the tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to include evaluation criteria in the notification applies only to European calls for tenders.
In national procurement procedures, evaluation criteria must only be included in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
If the procurement documents are prepared by authorized independent experts, German regulations require the exclusion of any information that would help identify specific interested companies, architects or designers.
The reason is to prevent individual construction companies from obtaining any additional information or other unacceptable competitive advantages without the knowledge of the contracting authority through contacts with these independent experts.
This is also why independent experts are not allowed to publish tender documents and make them accessible for screening.
The contracting authority is the only contact point for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, after the conclusion of the original contract, there will almost always be a need for additional work or modification of existing works.
The legislation of both countries makes it possible to award a multi-work contract within a certain limit to the same supplier without the need to publish and implement a new procurement procedure.
The volume of multipliers under European and German legislation must not exceed 50% of the original value of the contract.
Moreover, multi-machinery must be necessary and have the character of a specific component of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied for the award of multipliers.
This may lead to a risk of a greater time lag in the execution of the contract and the possible integration of a new entity into an already running project.
Where a multi-work contract is awarded to the supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision preventing suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified works or multipliers.
Calculations shall be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to multipliers, there may be minor works during construction, i.e. works that were not needed.
In the Czech Republic it is not possible to compensate for each other.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product neutral invitation to tender
In isolated cases, the specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards but were replaced by EU standards in the meantime.
In some cases, the specifications included particulars requiring a particular trademark, e.g. for a system of resistance of public inscriptions, for preventing water penetration or for noise cladding, although this was not needed.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantitative specifications divided into items
The BRH has not, even in one case, recorded a check of the procurement procedures for non-compliance with the general requirements for the invitation to tender for supplies and services in accordance with the quantitative specifications broken down into items.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the audited cases, tendering documents were fully prepared prior to the call for tenders.
However, in various cases the documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure, taking into account changes in items or in the timetable.
In isolated cases, the procurement documents contained works and supplies that were insecure.
One procurement document for demolition works contained almost exclusively such items.
Division of contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for not dividing deliveries into parts has not been demonstrated.
For the award criteria, BRH did not find any shortcomings.
Inclusion of names of authorized independent experts
Many documents listed interested authorized experts.
The names were given on the opening pages of documents or on plans or annexed opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact the explicitly appointed architect with their procurement questions.
In the case of land construction projects, these cases were found primarily in the procurement documents, where self-employed experts were entrusted with the processing of the specifications.
The contracting authorities indicated that they did not have sufficient personnel capacity to carry out detailed screening of the procurement documents.
SAO
Technical specifications and product neutral invitation to tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 flats, there was a reference directly to specific manufacturers and product brands.
The contracting authority did not allow the use of other similar product types or justify requiring the delivery of specific products.
For the construction of the facilities of the university, the procurement documents included references to specific companies and specific product labels.
Overall list of specifications and sub-specifications
In the case of the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority has not clearly defined the quantity and type of work, supplies or services required in the statement of dimensions in the procurement documents and has established only the cubic metre space for each building as a unit of measurement.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of the road circuit, the tender documents for the restricted procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in project documentation caused by the expansion of the scope of the contract and the change in technical conditions.
For the construction of social buildings, the procurement documentation had defects that forced the creation of multipliers (there was no precise focus, insufficient technical surveys were carried out, drawings were missing).
The procurement documents for the construction of the large library listed interior elements only descriptively, without specifying qualitative product requirements.
The supply of equipment for the interior of the building was provided by subcontractors who acquired some extra and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for the award of the contract
In the Czech Republic there is no obligation to award a contract in parts.
The analysis of three large road structures during the construction of the road circuit showed that when they were awarded in open procedure in parts (separately after selected buildings), savings of up to 790 million could be achieved. CZK from the achieved price of public contracts 15,508 million. CZK.
For example, when six buildings were awarded as a separate part of the contract, a saving of 62.5 million could be achieved.
CZK 25% of the contracted price and a saving of CZK 287.8 million could be achieved when awarding 20 bridges as a separate part of the public contract. CZK, i.e. also 25%.
In the construction of the roads in the procurement documents, the contracting authority established qualifying criteria that restricted the participation of small suppliers in the procurement procedure.
In the procurement conditions of an open procedure for a supplier for one construction of a motorway, the contracting authority requested, inter alia, the practice of a supplier consisting in the implementation of at least three road constructions with a financial volume of every more than 700 miles. CZK, completed or operational during the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that fulfil the condition of completion of construction over 700 miles. CZK in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of another highway construction in open procedure included six separate buildings for which the contracting authority requested a minimum annual volume of construction work in the last three years of EUR 12 billion.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. €2.5 billion out of €3.7 billion) in the period of 2008-2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually either in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of CZK 13.2 billion there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that limiting market potential could lead to a large increase in costs.
Multiplication area
In two cases, the contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of multi-works during the construction of the road circuit and did not award any of the procedures specified in the Act.
The value of the pre-approved multipliers in one case was 1 591.4 million. CZK excluding VAT (increase by 21%) and in the latter case CZK 4,135,8 million. CZK excluding VAT (increase by 91%).
The main cause of the multiplication was the ambiguous definition of the quantity and type of work required.
In the case of the construction of a university facility, the contracting authority did not include in the procurement documents certain works and supplies, the necessity of which was already known for the completion and approval of the construction when the contract was awarded.
For these works and deliveries he concluded additions to the contract for the work with the contractor with an increase in the price, in the negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of the work exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Palace of Justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a procurement procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by 50,5 million. CZK.
Unit prices of additional works determined without competition were significantly higher than comparable works carried out by the same supplier.
Other findings
The SAO found on a selected sample of buildings that the average construction costs for the construction of one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic are 355.0 million. CZK.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometre of the four-lane motorway is 260.0 million. CZK.
This comparison suggests that buying a motorway in the Czech Republic is more costly than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions relating to procurement documents are almost identical in both countries and comply with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and the prevention of corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow candidates to calculate costs and prepare their bids.
Moreover, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and protects the contracting authority against the need to enter multi-machinery.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on procurement documents are complemented by other rules designed to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and handling.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved by publishing procurement criteria.
In both countries, specification requirements are generally met.
However, in some cases deficiencies have been identified which could lead to a reduction in competition.
At the same time, the rule in force in Germany requiring the subdivision of large construction projects into parts seems appropriate for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, the award of contracts for large-volume projects without partial award or division into large-volume parts can limit the range of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
As a result of the strict requirement for references of tenderers, which is applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of tenderers for road works, the fact that public contracts are awarded only to a small range of tenderers does not give small and medium-sized firms a chance to compete.
A limited number of tenderers also increases the risk of price fixation and other illegal conventions.
As regards the award and reimbursement of additional multi-work or modified work contracts, regulatory approaches in both countries differ.
Where the value of additional work under European and German law does not exceed 50% of the value of the original contract, multi-works may be awarded without a procurement procedure and its notification.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% applies.
IRING segmentISintent  EU blank
This limit is often exceeded by the contracting authority.
IRING segmentISintent  EU blank
If the limit of 20% is exceeded, the multipliers must be entered on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
Dividing work into parts avoids limiting competition as well as resulting adverse consequences.
Determination of the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU limits
By determining the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential suppliers to be admitted to the competition.
The contracting authority may leave the range of potential suppliers unlimited or limit the range to the criteria specified.
A European procurement procedure must be conducted in the case of large project works whose value exceeds or exceeds the limits set by the EU.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
When choosing the type of management, the contracting authority shall meet in particular the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimated total cost of the contract for the required works exceeds or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million ($125 million). CZK), an open or limited procurement procedure at European level is always required.
In exceptional cases, negotiated procedures or competitive dialogue shall be permitted.
Where a construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may, to a limited extent, deviate from the European procurement procedure by issuing a national procedure where the total value of the part concerned is below €1.0 million ($25 million). CZK) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the estimated value of the entire contract.
Dividing a construction project or deliberately estimating its costs too low in order to avoid the essential obligation to launch a European procurement procedure is not permissible.
Choosing the type of management is very important for the prevention of corruption, as restricting competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, a detailed justification for the type of procedure chosen is an important part of the procurement documentation, in particular in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU legal requirements for procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of proceedings are allowed only if the conditions prescribed by law are met.
Unlike in the Czech Republic, the relevant German regulation of budgetary law and corruption prevention principles prefers open proceedings or a public invitation to participate in proceedings.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted procedures, are admissible only exceptionally, where permitted by legislation.
Such exceptions shall be justified and documented.
The principle of open procedure preference is intended to ensure that restrictions on competition are prevented to the maximum extent and that the opportunity for manipulation is limited (specific candidates selected in advance).
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to preventing corruption.
A correct estimate of the expected price of the contract is an important basis for a decision on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
When estimating the cost of building projects, contracting entities in Germany may rely on budgetary and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These have been compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for construction types, building units, building parts and sub-works.
Based on such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the value of the contract of the planned project or works.
Only a few construction administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be extended at national level.
Contracting entities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable buildings to determine the expected costs of public works contracts.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimate of the entity financing the project or is based on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Standardisation measures are still at an early stage.
Based on the anti-corruption programme 2011-2012, the relevant ministries are to develop a database of the usual prices of standardised commodities.
The application of the price system to determine the expected price of a contract is determined by a new regulation ordering the establishment of the subject-matter in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on works, materials and products and other data in order to calculate the necessary costs and to determine unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued prescriptive prices for transport works in order to check them, which are regularly updated.
The database is intended to determine the price of the project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of the construction work preparation.
Main BRH control findings
Selection of the procurement procedure
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts in an open procedure or in the form of a public invitation to participate, thereby complying with the requirements of the budgetary and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of public tenders in the total volume of federal construction projects in the field of land construction was 59.5%.
In road construction, this proportion was 94.8%.
BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public competition was not sufficiently justified.
On the basis of information collected from more than 16 000 proceedings, the BRH has found that restricted proceedings have a clear disadvantage compared to public proceedings and public appeals, and restricted proceedings also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
IRING segmentISintent  EU blank
According to the BRH study, bidding prices in restricted procedures and negotiated procedures were 6% to 10% higher than in public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Regional Court of Auditors confirmed differences in the value for money obtained in public and restricted proceedings.
Indeed, a study by the Ministry of Construction found that prices in restricted procedures were twice as high as those in public proceedings.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects whose total contract value was estimated at more than €5 million ($125 million). The audited contracting entities usually prepared procurement plans specifying which works will be awarded in the European procurement procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the procurement plans, the share of work awarded under European rules has always exceeded 80% of total work.
There was no case of circumvention of the European management requirement in the form of an excessively low cost estimate.
However, in some cases the minimum share for pan-European management has not been achieved as a result of a calculation error.
In the course of carrying out the work, some contracting entities have illegally modified the award plan.
They did not justify these changes in any way and did not demonstrate that the minimum proportion of the European procedure was subsequently respected.
As a general rule, the proportion of European procedures under the project accounts was lower than that set out in the procurement plan.
If the expected value of construction project contracts exceeded the European limits, contracting authorities did not apply the European procedure to all individual sub-parts worth more than €1 million (EUR 25 million). CZK).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure, without giving any compelling reasons for the need to derogate from the requirement.
SAO
Selection of the type of procurement procedure
In the case of several large construction projects (e.g. construction of a road section, equipment for a major sporting event and housing construction), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, although these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The distribution decreased the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded in a less demanding mode or even outside the legal regime.
Methods for calculating the expected price
The price standards applicable to road construction projects have not yet been an effective tool for determining the expected price.
They were based on the prices presented in the completed procedures, which were, however, burdened by non-economic and excessive profits for suppliers.
The SAO found that the actual prices for the three parts of the road circuit were about 37% lower than these price standards.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the standards in relation not only to past prices, but also to other comparable markets and price developments.
For land construction, the expected price was based on the budget of the project carried out by the architects or the investor estimate.
The actual price then differed considerably from the expected price.
The expected cost of building a large library was twice as high as the contract price, but increased by 54 million in the course of implementation. CZK, i.e. by 27%.
Similarly, for the construction of the depositary of this library, the contractual price was 33% lower than the expected price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the expected price and increased further in the course of implementation.
The building of a large library was 400 miles away. CZK, i.e. by 22%. For the construction of the Palace of Justice, the actual price during the construction process increased disproportionately (by 139%) compared to the increase of functional areas (only by 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of type of procurement procedure and on the respect of European limits in their national legislation, so the respective national arrangements of both countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public proceedings in Germany is stricter than the European regulation and the current national regulation in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a restricted procurement procedure, a public procedure or a public invitation to participate gives the possibility for more competitors to participate in the procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of tenders that guarantee the best effect of value for money.
The prevention of corruption is greatly enhanced as the range of candidates is not restricted by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which gives room for manipulation and price fixation between tenderers.
The studies confirmed the advantages of the German provision on the preference for open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to award contracts in open or restricted procedures.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a prohibition for contracting authorities to restrict the number of tenderers in restricted procedures.
The restricted procedure has now come very close to an open procedure.
In Germany, the contracting authority is largely satisfied with the requirement of an open procedure preference or a public call for competition, while in the Czech Republic some projects were divided in violation of the law in order to circumvent the requirement to select the relevant type of procurement procedure.
However, in the case of certain projects carried out in Germany, the European limits were not respected, resulting in a lack of a minimum share in pan-European procedures.
A satisfactory system has not yet been put in place in the Czech Republic to facilitate the correct calculation of the expected costs of construction works and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting authorities should, through more extensive controls, ensure that the requirements for the selection of the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are met, or should create the conditions necessary to enable the expected costs to be calculated correctly.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation
Where contracts are awarded under a European procedure, the contracting authority shall publish a procurement report.
Preliminary information and procurement notices are an important basis for European competition.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
By means of prior notices, the contracting authority shall provide the basic specifications of the projects envisaged.
Prior notifications shall be required for projects where the total cost estimate is equal to or greater than the European limit and where the contracting authority wishes to shorten the time limit for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority is obliged to publish a contract notice with detailed information for all public contracts which require a European procedure.
The information shall be binding upon the contracting entity after publication.
Notices shall include, inter alia, specifications, time limits for the procedure and for the completion of the contract, specific requirements and selection criteria and their weights.
The common contract dictionary shall be used to describe the subject matter of the contract.
Preliminary information and contract notices are published in the EU in the Official Journal of the EU and on the contracting authority's profile.
In order to prevent discrimination against foreign applicants, notifications at national level should not contain any additional information and should not be published before sending the text of the notification to the EU Publications Office.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in prior notification may distort competition or even ease handling.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU provisions on preliminary information and procurement notices in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, preliminary information and notices must also be published in the case of contracts for which no European procedure is required.
The procurement procedure may not be initiated more than one month after the pre-notification has been sent.
The publication of preliminary information may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, preliminary information is not required in cases where a European procedure is not necessary.
In the case of national proceedings, the public call must be published on the federal government's website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted proceedings without competition from participants, the notice must be published where the estimated price of the contract is equal to or greater than € 25,000 (CZK 625 000).
Preliminary notifications must explain all planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small contracts in Germany.
The division of projects into smaller parts is not so frequent in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Prior notification
The contracting authorities published a preliminary notice of projects which included work to be commissioned in the European procedure.
In some open procedures, they shortened the time limit, although the number of days between sending the prior notification and sending the contract notice was less than 52 days.
In the case of other shortened proceedings, the annual deadlines for prior information have expired and should be renewed.
In many cases, the prior notification did not indicate the expected division of the works into several parts.
Notice of initiation of the procurement procedure
Some notifications contained codes from the common dictionary that deviated from the content of the prior notification or did not specify precisely the work to be carried out.
In isolated cases, notifications at national level included more information, such as on delivery dates, or were published before a notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority has hired an external company to publish a national notice.
It has published a notice on its platform, the use of which has been charged.
However, the contracting authority did not check whether this company must comply with the requirement to send further notifications to a free Internet government portal.
The sample found that only a third of the notifications were actually published on the government portal.
SAO
Notice of initiation of the procurement procedure
During the construction of the wastewater treatment plant, the contracting authority did not set criteria for the construction contractors to reduce the large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure in the contract contract notice.
The contracting authority was unable to substantiate the method of selecting (reducing) the evaluation tenders.
The number of tenders was reduced by previous legislation.
In the construction of a road circuit, the contracting authority has not defined in the notice of initiation of the restricted procedure or in the qualification documentation the values according to which it will assess the level of compliance with the qualification criteria or the number of candidates to be invited.
The contracting authority assessed the fulfilment of the qualification in such a way that all six candidates in the restricted procedure fulfilled it, but excluded one of them with the fulfilment of the last sixth place.
The contracting authority's procedure was not transparent as it did not disclose in advance what qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publication of calls
In the case of construction modifications in former military injuries, the contracting authority did not publish a written invitation to tender in violation of the law.
Conclusions and recommendations/summary
A two-step information process with preliminary information and publication of a notice of initiation is an important tool for meeting the transparency requirements and strengthening the competitive environment applied in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish preliminary information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and by strengthening this obligation in Germany in cases of restricted procedures, both countries increased transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
Contrary to the open procedure, limited procurement procedures do not allow all interested potential tenderers to participate.
